  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_0878e006-d139-4df1-87a5-8fe5eb9be28d'
      });
    }

<span id="freeTextContainer962809370077654686">Trapped in a dead-end job in his Ohio hometown, watching the girl of his dreams move on to a glamorous new life in a big city—Donald McDougal’s aimlessness has held him back for a long time. When a lightning strike grants him superhuman powers, he jumps at his chance to finally be somebody. But the new abilities and the pursuit of superheroic fame come with a price tag, an</span>
  <span id="freeText962809370077654686" style="display:none">Trapped in a dead-end job in his Ohio hometown, watching the girl of his dreams move on to a glamorous new life in a big city—Donald McDougal’s aimlessness has held him back for a long time. When a lightning strike grants him superhuman powers, he jumps at his chance to finally be somebody. But the new abilities and the pursuit of superheroic fame come with a price tag, and it may not be one he can afford.<br /><br />This wry debut is at once a fanboy’s homage to the history of superhero storytelling in America and a keen-eyed satire of those same stories, raising questions about race and privilege that are becoming impossible to ignore.</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer5119599544101547922"><i>***Note: I received a copy curtesy of Netgalley and Lanternfish Press in exchange for an honest review.</i><br/><br/>Unfortunately, I didn’t quite manage to relate to this book ot its characters, maybe because of the slang, maybe because of the one too many murky comics references, maybe because of the insistence on using metafiction too often and using the same technique in almost all chapters (starting with a challenging situation and then going back to explain how he got there). <br/>I should have liked it, as </span>
  <span id="freeText5119599544101547922" style="display:none"><i>***Note: I received a copy curtesy of Netgalley and Lanternfish Press in exchange for an honest review.</i><br /><br />Unfortunately, I didn’t quite manage to relate to this book ot its characters, maybe because of the slang, maybe because of the one too many murky comics references, maybe because of the insistence on using metafiction too often and using the same technique in almost all chapters (starting with a challenging situation and then going back to explain how he got there). <br />I should have liked it, as I enjoy humorous, ironic superheroes who make mistakes, talk to and make fun of themselves (see Deadpool for example), but something was off for me. Oh, and the ending was quite a mystery for me..<br /><br />It has, though, lots of funny moments and a quite interesting story, so maybe check it out if the synopsis appeals to you. <blockquote>
  <i> “What the hell?” I said aloud. If there’s a checklist of superhero habits, talking to yourself has to be on there somewhere. I once attributed it to lazy writing in the comics when you saw super-whoever speeding across the sky with speech bubbles instead of thought bubbles, conversing with themself about whatever catastrophic event they were on their way to stop. The bad comic writers had it right, though.<br /><br />There’s anything and everything you could ever hope for, True Believer, as long as you keep listening. Please? </i>
</blockquote></span>

  <span id="freeText2205899271659416654" style="display:none">Imagine your funny, fallible, deeply geeky friend is stuck by lightning and gets superpowers... and well, wrote you a book about it. <br /><br />In this debut, Eskew takes you along with his hero as he stumbles through what it takes to be a superhero, without falling into the well-trod traps of being overly idealistic or, alternately, super gritty. This hero doesn't always make super choices, and faces real life consequences from events as large as stopping a supervillain, or as small as eating all his friend's cereal. His world is as flawed as our own, and peppered with more geeky references than I probably got on my first reading. <br />This book is beautiful, strange, and most importantly, REAL. <br />Plus, did I mention it was funny?</span>

<div id="review_2906920988" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2906920988" />
    <a title="Jesse bowtiesandbooks " class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/85652414-jesse-bowtiesandbooks"><img alt="Jesse bowtiesandbooks " src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1535736310p2/85652414.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2906920988?book_show_action=true">Jul 22, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jesse bowtiesandbooks " class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jesse bowtiesandbooks " href="/user/show/85652414-jesse-bowtiesandbooks">Jesse bowtiesandbooks </a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2906920988" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15870730498320090070"><b> “Heroes are only heroic in the full light of day. In the dark, where all things are beautiful and unkind, the gray lot of us are bare.” </b><br/><br/> Opportunity in the form of a catastrophic lightning strike changes Donald McDougal’s mundane life in unimaginable ways. Before the accident, he was laboring at a job he couldn’t stand, recovering from the loss of his girlfriend who broke up with him without a second glance backward. Donald is more than just mediocre, he’s positively forgettable. But when fate </span>
  <span id="freeText15870730498320090070" style="display:none"><b> “Heroes are only heroic in the full light of day. In the dark, where all things are beautiful and unkind, the gray lot of us are bare.” </b><br /><br /> Opportunity in the form of a catastrophic lightning strike changes Donald McDougal’s mundane life in unimaginable ways. Before the accident, he was laboring at a job he couldn’t stand, recovering from the loss of his girlfriend who broke up with him without a second glance backward. Donald is more than just mediocre, he’s positively forgettable. But when fate intervenes, Donald wakes up from the lightning strike in the hospital: with gray hair, a killer body, and the ability to manipulate electricity. Tales of the Astonishing Black Spark is about his journey as a superhero, in pursuit of fame and fortune he believes is rightly his, which will cost him more than he can imagine.<br /><br />This book is a mosaic of geek culture, political conversations, and a personal coming of age. I loved how reading it reminded me of why I fell in love with comic books in the first place; stories that showed us even the most average individual might possess incredible greatness. Astonishing Black Spark is full of nerdy references and homages to powerful, inspiring comic book characters, writers, and directors who have united people across the world. I loved the references to Firefly, Patton Oswalt, Jack Kirby, Green Lantern. Loved the witty dialogue and banter between Donald and his best friends, Kat and Paul. <br /><br /> It provided a level of logistics that I look for in my comic books - explanations of how superpowers actually might work, of what life would realistically be like with them. I loved the level of realism in such a fantastical story. I also thoroughly enjoyed the conversations about race, especially in how people treat Donald as a black superhero, always reminding him of his blackness if he forgets; reminding us that even with superpowers, black people are always going to be seen as black. First and foremost. <br /><br /> This book made me laugh. It made me miss the kid I used to be; buried in comics, watching X-Men reruns, cheering for Rogue, looking in the mirror and imagining I was Colossus. My favorite part of this book, hands down? Was how it reminded me why I fell in love with science fiction and comics in the first place. Why it will always be my first and most pure love. <br /><br /> My criticisms just boil down to personal preference. Donald speaks directly to the reader in a way that pulls from the story. Instead of making me feel more connected to the narrative, it reminds me that I’m reading a book and makes it difficult for me to be immersed. Eskew likely wrote it that way as an ode to the many comics that break the fourth wall, but that technique has never been one for me. I also wished there were more lush descriptions of the setting; the atmosphere of a book is everything to me and is the key for me being able to envision the surroundings and place myself in the story. <br /><br />	Overall, I STRONGLY suggest this book for those who love comics, sci fi, and overall geekiness. I look forward to what Charlie creates in the future. 3.5/5 stars</span>
  <a data-text-id="15870730498320090070" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2451704061" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2451704061" />
    <a title="lib" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/45817583-lib"><img alt="lib" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1601728941p2/45817583.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2451704061?book_show_action=true">Jul 09, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="lib" class="user" itemprop="url" name="lib" href="/user/show/45817583-lib">lib</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2451704061" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9196202097733409684"><br/>note: copy provided by NetGalley and Lanternfish Press for free in return for an honest review<br/><br/><br/>This is the tale of a man who got struck by lightning, and--you guessed it--got electro super powers. What is perhaps the least original origin story is woven into a story that is, funnily enough, quite original. It takes place in what I can only call the most accurate depiction of the world we live in today but with one single twist: superheroes. Which, oddly enough, happens to be a very large twist.<br/><br/>Th</span>
  <span id="freeText9196202097733409684" style="display:none"><br />note: copy provided by NetGalley and Lanternfish Press for free in return for an honest review<br /><br /><br />This is the tale of a man who got struck by lightning, and--you guessed it--got electro super powers. What is perhaps the least original origin story is woven into a story that is, funnily enough, quite original. It takes place in what I can only call the most accurate depiction of the world we live in today but with one single twist: superheroes. Which, oddly enough, happens to be a very large twist.<br /><br />The main character, Donald, is the typical, struggling, American man. He’s stuck in a shitty job, he’s got 99 problems and most of them have to do with one particular girl, and he just happens to be a super… hero?<br /><br /><br />Eskew creates a world so scarily similar to our own, with its big ol’ issues and its great big beauties. It’s written in the style of a memoir which is super cool. It read like nonfiction other than the whole, you know, minor superhero thing. The voice that is created is eerily believable and the style through which the events are expressed makes everything seem as if it actually happened. The fake-memoir method of writing reminded me of “What We Do in the Shadows”. It’s a hilarious mockumentary about vampires in New Zealand that takes place in a realistic world but has a similar-ish paranormal twist to The Tales of the Astonishing Black Spark.<br /><br />Donald’s voice is real and self aware. It hits on topics of racial inequality and raises questions about current racialized issues such as social justice campaigning in the modern world as well as how history idealized racial issues. These ideas shine through at two particularly powerful moments.*<br /><br />a) “My smoking habit could play as metaphor for any Black Power movement after the 80s: not fully committed, reluctantly active.”<br />This is honestly the best line in the entire novel. A completely accurate and well founded nudge at the current state of social justice movements. Although this is aimed specifically at racial equality movements I do think it’s applicable to pretty much any civil rights campaign that we see in the media today.<br /><br />b) “I’m aware that two more racially aimed comments will officially land this little memoir in the dusty African-American section of your local bookstore.”<br />This was a really witty remark that hit perfectly with my sense of humor. I think it’s ironic because although Eskew is kind of criticising this, because of how much race is brought up in the novel, the book may actually be deemed African-American literature.<br />And you bet your ass that there were faaaaaaaaaaaarrrrrrrrrrr more than just two racially aimed comments that fell after this statement. So much so that it became excessive, repetitive, and, to be blunt, pretty damn annoying.<br /><br />I could go on and on about the ideas that both these quotations raise but I don’t want this book review to turn into a statement on current social issues so I’ll leave it at that.<br /><br />*Because these quotes are taken from an ARC it is not guaranteed that these will appear in this exact form (or at all) in the final released version of the novel.<br /><br />There was also a training montage in this book which also gets a thumbs up from me. I love a good training montage and this one was pretty lit. Not quite Rocky Balboa lit, but lit all the same.<br /><br /><br />Eskew is constantly making points about the hypocrisy and ridiculousness of some aspects of culture, society, and religion through hyperbole and exaggeration. However, sometimes it goes from satirical and clever to a little bit too ridiculous, too unrealistic, and kinda (very) cringy. Tries to be a little bit too “down with the kids these days”. Reminds me of r/fellowkids. If you know what I’m talking about, bless your soul.<br /><br />The book hops from the really mundane (something I don’t tend to enjoy reading about (this is why I don’t really read contemporary)) to super ridiculous crazy ass shit. It’s kind of cool and interesting but also jarring and abrupt, which isn’t great.<br /><br />As I stated previously, the origin story of the fabled Black Spark is the most unimaginative shit I’ve ever read in my life. The unoriginality of it really disappointed me, especially because everything else about this book screams “fresh” and “new” and “current”. There are so many fun and decently plausible (well, actually not plausible at all considering that there is no such thing as super powers but let’s ignore that fact for the moment) ways he could have developed his powers. Like sticking a fork in a toaster, or acting out that one scene in Jurassic Park where the kid in on the electrocuted fence and he goes zap zap and even though I’m not explaining this well at all you know exactly what I’m talking about. Eskew really let me down on this one.<br /><br /><br />I wasn’t a huge fan of this book. I think, if handled with a little more tact, it could have been amazing. It brought up important topics and dealt with new and interesting ideas, but ultimately the repetitiveness and ridiculousness left me more than a little disappointed.<br /><br /><br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="9196202097733409684" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2391639814?book_show_action=true">Aug 20, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Dan" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Dan" href="/user/show/71374712-dan">Dan</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2391639814" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3294823645330908563">I received a galley copy of this title prior to release.<br/><br/>Donald, a Midwestern, African American geek gets struck by lightning, becomes a superhero, superhero things happen. Comics, and superhero books and movies are not my personal preference, but as a YA Librarian, I have selected this book for a number of reasons. 1. Superhero fiction, and prose adaptations of graphic novels are very popular right now. 2. This is a African American character written by an author of color. The world needs more o</span>
  <span id="freeText3294823645330908563" style="display:none">I received a galley copy of this title prior to release.<br /><br />Donald, a Midwestern, African American geek gets struck by lightning, becomes a superhero, superhero things happen. Comics, and superhero books and movies are not my personal preference, but as a YA Librarian, I have selected this book for a number of reasons. 1. Superhero fiction, and prose adaptations of graphic novels are very popular right now. 2. This is a African American character written by an author of color. The world needs more of this, the YA community is demanding more of it. (See The Hate U Give, Children of Blood and Bone, etc.) 3. The whole book just seems very "real". Nothing in the story relies too heavily on tropes (except maybe the origin story) and Donald makes his messy inexperienced way through the story in a very relatable way.<br /><br />Parts of the book come across as a little disjointed and need some polish. There are a lot of pop culture references and geek speak that may not stand the test of time. The story is also interrupted regularly by passages like "This is the Tale of the _____ ______ ______...." which would make for better chapter titles than it does as a way of developing prose.<br /><br />All in all its a fun read that will resonate with teens, young adults, and super hero fans and an interesting first novel from an author with a unique perspective.</span>
  <a data-text-id="3294823645330908563" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_2839822048" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2839822048" />
    <a title="Ellie White" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/91833788-ellie-white"><img alt="Ellie White" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1559226144p2/91833788.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2839822048?book_show_action=true">May 30, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ellie White" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ellie White" href="/user/show/91833788-ellie-white">Ellie White</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2839822048" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11895237346164096395">As someone who has never read a comic book and has only the most basic knowledge of popular superheroes, I was not sure what to expect from this book. Fortunately, the plot was consistently engaging, and I found the narrator funny and relatable. I’m a former resident of Columbus, Ohio so I caught on to a lot of the sometimes thinly veiled references to local places. (Also, thank you, Spark, for saying something nice about the open mic poets.) I’m also familiar with NYC, so that was another way I</span>
  <span id="freeText11895237346164096395" style="display:none">As someone who has never read a comic book and has only the most basic knowledge of popular superheroes, I was not sure what to expect from this book. Fortunately, the plot was consistently engaging, and I found the narrator funny and relatable. I’m a former resident of Columbus, Ohio so I caught on to a lot of the sometimes thinly veiled references to local places. (Also, thank you, Spark, for saying something nice about the open mic poets.) I’m also familiar with NYC, so that was another way I was able to connect with the story. My one and only complaint with this book was the ending. It felt anticlimactic, and I just didn’t get it. <br /><br />On a different note, I brought up this book to my partner, who is far geekier than I, and within about two minutes he was speaking an entirely different language and referencing all these fictional characters I had never heard of. What this confirmed for me was my suspicion that there were a ton of references I was missing that were probably very intentional and cleverly done. I imagine a reader with more knowledge of superheroes would find quite a few things to appreciate in this book that I could not. But we can’t choose our passions. And overall, this was still a fun read. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="11895237346164096395" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2879475387" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2879475387" />
    <a title="Nat" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/9110882-nat"><img alt="Nat" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1376109951p2/9110882.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2879475387?book_show_action=true">Jul 01, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Nat" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Nat" href="/user/show/9110882-nat">Nat</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2879475387" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6234858903130718253">It was a fun take on super heroes with the tongue and cheek humor. The author is quite clever. This book appeals to those who grew up loving super heroes and are fond of meta fiction</span>

<div id="review_2642587902" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2642587902" />
    <a title="Pam" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/55118282-pam"><img alt="Pam" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/u_50x66-632230dc9882b4352d753eedf9396530.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2642587902?book_show_action=true">Dec 28, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Pam" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Pam" href="/user/show/55118282-pam">Pam</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2642587902" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer312708621594083803"><b>4.5 stars</b><br/><br/>I would rate this as 4, but I want to bump it up for a few reasons - one, uniqueness to me. I've never read a <i>novel</i> about a superhero. What a delight. Two, it was easy to read, and I knew going in that this was the author's first novel [as far as I know], so I'm just darn impressed. And three, I just really liked it and I'd like everyone to read it please so we can talk about it.<br/><br/>The cover itself shares some of what the core of the novel is going to be: a Black superhero struggles to be </span>
  <span id="freeText312708621594083803" style="display:none"><b>4.5 stars</b><br /><br />I would rate this as 4, but I want to bump it up for a few reasons - one, uniqueness to me. I've never read a <i>novel</i> about a superhero. What a delight. Two, it was easy to read, and I knew going in that this was the author's first novel [as far as I know], so I'm just darn impressed. And three, I just really liked it and I'd like everyone to read it please so we can talk about it.<br /><br />The cover itself shares some of what the core of the novel is going to be: a Black superhero struggles to be seen, and not as "the Black superhero." I liked the pace of the novel, which goes from future to past perspectives, or hints at what's coming later on, similar to the way a superhero movie might say "how did we get here?" and then "rewind" for you. In a way, it felt like watching a superhero movie, in the best way possible.<br /><br />My biggest complaint is that there are references to our current late capitalist society where the names of companies/institutions are slightly mangled. It's done enough in the book that it feels more trite than clever, but I'm not sure how I would change it, so could be a matter of taste. In the same vein, some jokes are a bit too "look, a joke!", for example "American Collective for Resolving Overtly Negative Yowled Misconducts" aka ACRONYM.<br /><br />Still giving it 5 stars though, because it was interesting, unique, and a bit dark. Great work.</span>
  <a data-text-id="312708621594083803" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2394492217" />
    <a title="Barbara Barrow" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/37206053-barbara-barrow"><img alt="Barbara Barrow" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1536971560p2/37206053.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2394492217?book_show_action=true">Oct 12, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Barbara Barrow" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Barbara Barrow" href="/user/show/37206053-barbara-barrow">Barbara Barrow</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2394492217" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11039105457886123011">This book about a young man-turned-superhero is both playful and serious. Douglas McDougal is working at Stereo Hutt and mourning his lost relationship with his ex when he is hit by a lightning strike that grants him superpowers. He competes to become a member of a national superhero association called the American Collective for Resolving Overtly Negative Yowled Misconducts, a process that puts his close relationships under strain and exposes him to intense national scrutiny. The book tackles i</span>
  <span id="freeText11039105457886123011" style="display:none">This book about a young man-turned-superhero is both playful and serious. Douglas McDougal is working at Stereo Hutt and mourning his lost relationship with his ex when he is hit by a lightning strike that grants him superpowers. He competes to become a member of a national superhero association called the American Collective for Resolving Overtly Negative Yowled Misconducts, a process that puts his close relationships under strain and exposes him to intense national scrutiny. The book tackles issues like exploitative advertising, media culture, racism, class division, white privilege, and more. My comic / superhero knowledge is limited, but I still found this book accessible and very enjoyable. In particular, I enjoyed the narrator's friendships with the writer / activist Paul and the artist Kat, as well as his recollections of his father. I also liked how the book described the little everyday details of being a superhero: figuring out the perfect superhero outfit, learning that superhero spandex is so snug that the only snack you can fit in your pocket is an Airhead, the forks in your apartment dancing from your sparky energy whenever you feel frustrated or nervous. </span>
  <a data-text-id="11039105457886123011" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

For the social justice focused geeks out there, this books is RIGHT up your alley. If you are neither a geek nor all that aware of the world around us in the States right now, I think you can still enjoy this book</span>
  <span id="freeText14716920990171083416" style="display:none">This debut novel from Eskew is super fun for the geeks out there with plenty of deep references to sci-fi and fantasy culture. The book points to tropes from the history of superhero stories while adding a fresh, 'woke' twist by working through those tropes with a Black main character. For the social justice focused geeks out there, this books is RIGHT up your alley. If you are neither a geek nor all that aware of the world around us in the States right now, I think you can still enjoy this book. The story is fun and interesting. I'm excited to see what Eskew brings next.</span>

<div id="review_2517888333" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2517888333" />
    <a title="Kristine" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4900654-kristine"><img alt="Kristine" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1298055050p2/4900654.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2517888333?book_show_action=true">Sep 04, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kristine" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kristine" href="/user/show/4900654-kristine">Kristine</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">

<span id="freeTextContainer16624984492892016290"><i>Tales of the Astonishing Black Spark by Charlie J. Eskew is a free NetGalley ebook that I read in late August.</i><br/><br/>Nerdy McNerdingtons, right off the bat. The Astonishing Black Spark of Ohio, aka Donald, outfitting himself, daring-do's, helpful friends, balancing good and bad press, and interacting/besting/romancing with other newly-found heroes.</span>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

      <span class='likeItContainer' id='like_container_review_2517888333'><a id="like_count_review_2517888333" rel="nofollow" href="/rating/voters/2517888333?resource_type=Review"><span class="likesCount">1 like</span></a>&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;<a rel="nofollow" class="jsLike like_it gr-button gr-button--small" href="https://www.goodreads.com/user/new?remember=true&amp;return_url=%2Fbook%2Fshow%2F40028529-tales-of-the-astonishing-black-spark">Like</a></span>

  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown" >
    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_2729689618" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2729689618" />
    <a title="R" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/18849271-r"><img alt="R" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1365895540p2/18849271.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2729689618?book_show_action=true">Mar 16, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="R" class="user" itemprop="url" name="R" href="/user/show/18849271-r">R</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2729689618" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10953673877006295986">There’s a lot to unpack here. Let’s start with the good stuff.<br/><br/>It’s clear that Charlie J. Eskew is a huge comic book fan who really did his research. The novel is full of heroes and villains with awesome powers that are exceedingly well-crafted and defined. You know exactly what each one can do and what their potential weaknesses are which makes for interesting fights and real stakes. You know that people can and will be hurt or even die. <br/><br/>The main character - Donald - is an electricity-based hero</span>
  <span id="freeText10953673877006295986" style="display:none">There’s a lot to unpack here. Let’s start with the good stuff.<br /><br />It’s clear that Charlie J. Eskew is a huge comic book fan who really did his research. The novel is full of heroes and villains with awesome powers that are exceedingly well-crafted and defined. You know exactly what each one can do and what their potential weaknesses are which makes for interesting fights and real stakes. You know that people can and will be hurt or even die. <br /><br />The main character - Donald - is an electricity-based hero and his powers in particular were clearly well thought-out. He’s not just shooting lightning at people. He’s manipulating electricity in humans, technology, and all sorts of other places. He’s also black, which I promise is not a weird thing to bring up.<br /><br />Some tales take heroes and have them be women, black, LGBTQ+, etc. but don’t have these traits shape them or the story in any noticeable way. Other tales, like this one, let those aspects of the character define a large part of their story. Donald is heavily defined by his skin tone because it’s heavily shaped who he is and how he’s lived his life and that makes for an interesting read. So that I don’t give anything about this story away, I’m going to draw a parallel between this book and the early 2000’s TV show Static Shock. The show was about a black teenager who gained superpowers and most of the episodes were focused on normal superhero stuff. Static would fight villains, thwart evil plots, and save people. Occasionally, though, there’d be an episode where he had to deal with problems relating to racism or the fact that he lived in a low income area. I still remember an episode about a foster girl with super powers. These things weren’t the focus of the show, but they were key aspects that set it apart from other superhero shows airing at the time.<br /><br />It gave some people a role model they could relate too and gave people like me – a middle-class white girl – an enjoyable way to experience issues and situations that we’d never go through. I like to think that I learned a lot from that show and that’s the kind of story I love. I’m someone who wants to see other people’s perspectives, but doesn’t enjoy reading academic articles or realist books on the topics. It’s just not the way I’m wired. A superhero story that shows another perspective, though? Yes please!<br /><br />Tales of the Astonishing Black Spark does similar things to Static Shock. This isn’t a book about a black man’s struggle. It’s a book about a super hero who is black that takes some time to show the issues he faces because of that while also telling a superhero story. The biggest difference between the book and Static Shock is that Static was written in a manner that anyone could understand the issues raised if they wanted to. Tales of the Astonishing Black Spark sometimes raises issues in such a way that you sort of have to already know the issues in order to get what Donald’s dealing with or talking about. For example, he talks about why the black community isn’t a fan of another black superhero by giving the superheroes’ career history. I still have no idea why they don’t like him.<br /><br />To be fair, I’m not 100% sure who the audience is here. If this is aimed at the black community or people of color in general, then there’s no issues. It doesn’t need to explain itself. If this is aimed at comic fans in general, then some additional work is needed to explain the issues at hand for readers who don’t have the life experience to instantly know what the big issues are in the black community.<br /><br />That’s boarding on critical, so let’s jump over the border and get critical. There are two big items that kept this from being a five star book: the monologues and the non-linear story telling. I think the monologues were the bigger issue, so let’s start there.<br /><br />When I read a story, I want to form my own opinions. The author’s job is not to dissect what happens in the scenes they show us. Their job is to show me the tale and choose the right situations, words, and characters to get their point across without needing to explain anything in asides. This book has an annoying habit of doing the exact opposite. We’ll be given a scene and then the book will jump to Donald monologuing about what just happened. For example, we have several emotional scenes where he fights with friends. Instead of letting these scenes play out and then fade to black, the scene ends with the future Donald who is writing the book stepping in for a few paragraphs to talk about how he shouldn’t have done that. This largely removes the emotional impact a scene should have because, instead of being allowed to make our own judgment, Donald is telling us how to feel. It also draws you out of the story because it breaks the flow, reminding the reader this is all just a book they’re reading.<br /><br />That brings us to the other big issue: the non-liner style of the tale. Donald’s running commentary is part of this, but there’s more to it than that. The book is full of flash forwards and flashbacks. The flash forwards are by far the bigger issue, but let’s quickly address the flashbacks first.<br /><br />There are maybe eight in total and they are are by-and-large completely unnecessary. They really just serve to establish Donald’s childhood and recent past even though there’s nothing all that special that needs to be established. He’s a pretty average guy. For example, we meet his ex at one point and this is followed by a chapter recounting their relationship even though she’s an exceedingly minor character. Pretty much everything in this chapter could easily have been retold in brief conversations with his friends where he talks about running into her and they talk about why he should let that relationship go. However, other than the unnecessary nature of these scenes, they’re not a huge detriment to the story. They just needlessly slow it down.<br /><br />What is a huge detriment is the flash forwards.<br /><br />I lost count of how many of these there were and there are times where they completely ruin the story’s flow for no reason. There’s also two distinct type of flash-forwards. The first one is flashes to present/future Donald. AKA the Donald who is sitting in a coffee shop and writing the story. I have no idea why these were here. There’s nothing more jarring than reading about a super hero fight and then the next scene being Donald talking about ordering coffee or getting annoyed at the barista. It would have been far better to cut these out and just move chronologically from scene to scene.<br /><br />The other type of flash forward were these strange clip scenes that occasionally started chapters and then never came back. You’d have a chapter end with Donald going to a new place and then the next chapter would start with him talking to someone at the new place for about half a page. Then we’d jump back to Donald arriving at the place. You’d think these scene clips were teasers of what was to come, but I can’t think of a time where they came back and we got to see the rest of what happened. <br /><br />The monologues and the jumping around bloated the book and stole time from the actual story that it really needed. It was like watching an Avengers movie where half of the movie is spent with the characters pausing what’s going on to talk to the audience about what they’re thinking or to tell them to keep watching because it’s going to get good, only for the characters to then realize they need to honor the movie’s run time, so they fast forward through the rest of the scene that you actually wanted to see! I don’t get it and it got frustrating.<br /><br />If this book stuck to a more straight-forward telling of its story and let the readers make their own opinions, it would be a five star read because I loved the premise. I loved the plot. I liked many of the characters and their powers. I liked getting the perspective of a black man living in modern society given to me in a way that didn’t feel academic. There’s hints of a sequel and, if it’s more focused than this one, it could be amazing. There’s a lot of good stuff here to work with and, when he’s focused, Eskew is a good writer.<br /><br />Obviously I had some issues with the book, but I do recommend giving it a shot if you like heroes and non-standard viewpoints. There’s good stuff here. Just don’t expect this to be the type of book you get through in one sitting. When the novel was focused on the plot, it flew by. When it started jumping around, I had to start taking breaks.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10953673877006295986" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown" >
    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_2525512227" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2525512227" />
    <a title="David Lowrey" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/36080109-david-lowrey"><img alt="David Lowrey" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1413907478p2/36080109.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2525512227?book_show_action=true">Sep 10, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="David Lowrey" class="user" itemprop="url" name="David Lowrey" href="/user/show/36080109-david-lowrey">David Lowrey</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2525512227" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4686319982760821750">This is a science fiction/fantasy title in the genre of comic book superhero novelizations except that it is written by the Black Spark himself (Donald McDonald) in first person as if he were telling you a story on the bus. I found this book a change of pace from your typical SF/F titles because it is written tongue-in-cheek and mostly makes fun of itself. I enjoyed the novel and found the characters engaging and exciting, particularly the Black Spark himself.<br/><br/>Ronald McDonald, first of all, is bl</span>
  <span id="freeText4686319982760821750" style="display:none">This is a science fiction/fantasy title in the genre of comic book superhero novelizations except that it is written by the Black Spark himself (Donald McDonald) in first person as if he were telling you a story on the bus. I found this book a change of pace from your typical SF/F titles because it is written tongue-in-cheek and mostly makes fun of itself. I enjoyed the novel and found the characters engaging and exciting, particularly the Black Spark himself.<br /><br />Ronald McDonald, first of all, is black. This makes the novel refreshing in itself as there are so few black characters written as superheroes. Spark, as he call himself, is hit by lightening that should have killed him (like the Flash!) but somehow survives and retains the ability to control and propagate electrical charges from himself. At first, he doesn't know how to control this power, but he manages, in a stumbling manner, to defeat the 'Maverick Moralist,' who is an evil superpower hero who preaches people to death (I love it!), and he gains notoriety on You-Tube. He continues on to discover his powers with Nick, who later turns out to be a villain, design a costume with Kat, his best friend from grade school, and gradually join the only Avengers-type group in this world called the 'American Collective for Resolving Overtly Negative Yowled Misconduct.'<br /><br />The book winks at itself during the story and probably its strongest point is the snappy, snarky narrative of Donald telling us the story of his life. Here is an example of the snappy dialogue in the book:<br /><br /> "Okay, well I need your help," I said plopping down on her couch.<br /><br /> "How can a mild-mannered woman possibly be of use to the Midwest Marvel?"<br /><br /> "Mild-mannered? Where is she?" I said.<br /><br /> She tossed a pillow at my face. "You should be less of a douche to people your asking favors from."<br /><br /><br />The narrative is also laced with interrupting commentary in the form of "The tales of..." For example, "These are the astonishing tales of Finding X brand of napkins at X location on X day" and "These are the astonishing tales of Yahtzee." I found these interruptions amusing at time, but also tiresome at times. It is the author interjecting himself into the narrative, and it works, but just barely. <br /><br />On of his pals in the "American Collective..." is Patches, the Boy Band-aid. His name alone is a reason to read on. Patches has the ability to heal anyone of anything, but only once a day. Patches also like to smoke weed and there are some funny scenes of Donald stoned trying to control his electrical powers. Other characters, GRAVI-tina (who can control gravity), Asper (Who can fly), and the villain Nick (who can also control electricity) make for some funny reading.<br /><br />Overall, this is an entertaining book and a change of pace for anyone bored with reading a standard SF/F book or a mystery/thriller. Recommended. 4/5 stars. Read this and other book reviews at bookgeist.blogspot.com.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4686319982760821750" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2463725863" />
    <a title="Ed" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/73113374-ed"><img alt="Ed" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/u_50x66-632230dc9882b4352d753eedf9396530.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2463725863?book_show_action=true">Jul 20, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ed" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ed" href="/user/show/73113374-ed">Ed</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2463725863" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14340257781296112312">I thought this sounded like a fun book - a different take on the superhero origination theme. Then I started reading it....some of the background I could understand and relate to - the nerd in school. The rest can be summed up in a couple of quotes: a) “My smoking habit could play as metaphor for any Black Power movement after the 80s: not fully committed, reluctantly active.”<br/>b) “I’m aware that two more racially aimed comments will officially land this little memoir in the dusty African-American</span>
  <span id="freeText14340257781296112312" style="display:none">I thought this sounded like a fun book - a different take on the superhero origination theme. Then I started reading it....some of the background I could understand and relate to - the nerd in school. The rest can be summed up in a couple of quotes: a) “My smoking habit could play as metaphor for any Black Power movement after the 80s: not fully committed, reluctantly active.”<br />b) “I’m aware that two more racially aimed comments will officially land this little memoir in the dusty African-American section of your local bookstore.” Unfortunately, it got to the point where most of the story seemed to be "racially aimed comments". After the umpteenth anti-white stereotype, I had to put it down. I get your experience is different than mine, but I wouldn't write this about other races. I read to escape the current acrimony in the world, not bring it into my library.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14340257781296112312" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer5932790185887410012">Charlie J. Eskew has an odd, almost satyric look at superheroes. His Tales of the Astonishing Black Spark (paper from Lanternfish Press) is the tale of Donald McDougal, a young man in a dead end job who gets power over electricity after being struck by lightning. Trained by someone with the same powers, he applies to join the American Collective for Resolving Overtly Negative Yowled Misconducts when the first African American member quits. Eventually he is the second African American superhero. </span>
  <span id="freeText5932790185887410012" style="display:none">Charlie J. Eskew has an odd, almost satyric look at superheroes. His Tales of the Astonishing Black Spark (paper from Lanternfish Press) is the tale of Donald McDougal, a young man in a dead end job who gets power over electricity after being struck by lightning. Trained by someone with the same powers, he applies to join the American Collective for Resolving Overtly Negative Yowled Misconducts when the first African American member quits. Eventually he is the second African American superhero. He has to survive being killed and chasing away both his girl friends, before earning real income from the PantheUsa group and advertising. Lots of fun with a giggle.Review printed by Philadelphia Free Press</span>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('odetxSSSPV7K/3cHtzbuD4uKN6+JxKHZdC2Sx6Y0xJ7fEE1GpeeC1IYju3LC58VoLdxN6e0jx5nYAjzbNryOnQ==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('LPKSP2//B/qHPFUXGoXYnRas3ohiKgUTwoIAheGEI2BSNXK87oq4cMvgmWJvVPP6sPqkzgbNY1Nura6ZcQxpYw==')})
  }

But after his gift—or curse—almost killed his daughter, Midas relinquished The Touch forever. Ten years later, Princess Kora still bears the consequences of her father’s wish: her skin shines golden, rumors follow her everywhere she goes, and she harbors secret powers that are getting harder to hide.\n\nKora spends her days locked in the palace, concealed behind gloves and veils, trying to ignore the stares and gossip of courtiers. It isn’t until a charming young duke arrives that Kora realizes there may be someone out there who doesn’t fear her or her curse. But their courtship is disrupted when a thief steals precious items from the kingdom, leaving the treasury depleted and King Midas vulnerable. Thanks to her unique ability to sense gold, Kora is the only one who can track the thief down. As she sails off on her quest, Kora learns that not everything is what it seems—not thieves, not pirates, and not even curses. She quickly discovers that gold—and the power it brings—is more dangerous than she’d ever believed.\n\nMidas learned his lesson at a price.
